[2]This story, I really don't know where to start. But I've chosen a day as the beginning, on a certain Wednesday, at the parsonage, where people were having lunch. The conversation at the table was mostly unrelated to the current situation, but some of the things mentioned were quite insightful and would later influence the course of events.
[3]I cut a piece of boiled beef (by the way, it was tough) and returned to my seat. I said, "Whichever person killed Colonel Prothero made a contribution to the world." The emotion I felt while saying this was very unlike my clergyman's identity.
[4]My young nephew Dennis immediately responded:
[5]"If that guy is found lying in a pool of blood, you'll be accused of saying this. Mary will testify, won't she, Mary? She'll describe how you were brandishing the carving knife, with a determined look on your face, swearing to avenge the death."
[6]Mary is our maid, but for her, this job is just a stepping stone. Her real goal is to get a better position and earn a higher salary. She ignored Dennis's words and shouted sternly, "Vegetables!" She then pushed a cracked plate with force onto Dennis's face.
[7]My wife said sympathetically, "Is he really so annoying?"
[8]I didn't immediately respond because Mary slammed the vegetables onto the table and threw a mushy, unappetizing fruit pudding at my nose. I said, "No, thank you." But she still placed the dish "clank" on the table and left the room.
[9]"It's a shame I'm such a terrible housewife," my wife said with a hint of sincere regret.
[10]I agreed with her. My wife's name is Giselda[1], which is a suitable name for a clergyman's wife. But the suitability is only skin deep, as her personality is far from gentle.
[11] I've always thought that clergymen shouldn't marry. But why did I propose to Giselda after only 24 hours of knowing her? That's always been a mystery to me. I've always thought that marriage is a very serious matter, and before walking down the aisle, both parties must have a long period of thoughtful consideration, and the most important factor to consider is whether their tastes and hobbies are suitable for each other.
[12] Giselda is almost twenty years younger than me. She's a beautiful woman, stunningly beautiful. She seems to never take anything seriously. In fact, she's not suitable for me, especially for living together. She treats the parish as a joke to amuse herself. I've tried to change her mind, but I failed. So, I became more convinced that clergymen should be celibate. I hinted at similar ideas to her, but she just laughed.
[13] "Dear," I said, "if you could just pay a little more attention –"
[14] "Sometimes I do try," Giselda said, "but the result is always the opposite. Obviously, I'm not cut out to be a homemaker. Let's just leave the household chores to Mary. I'll just have to decide to live a less comfortable life and be willing to eat pickled food."
[15] "What about your husband, dear?" I said reproachfully, and to make my point more convincing, I quoted a proverb from the Bible: "She observes the household affairs…" [2]
[16] "You're lucky enough already," Giselda interrupted me immediately, "not being torn apart by lions or burned at the stake. Eating poorly, having more dust in the house, and seeing a few dead wasps - these things really don't deserve such a big fuss. Tell me more about Colonel Prosero. Anyway, early Christians were lucky, without church deacons to manage them."
[17] "Arrogant, beastly old man!" Dennis said. "No wonder his first wife left him."
[18] "What else could she do?" my wife said.
[19] "Giselda," I said sternly, "I won't allow you to say that."
[20] "Dear," my wife said affectionately, "tell me about this man! What happened? Was it because Mr. Hois every minute waving, nodding, and making the sign of the cross on his chest?"
[21] Mr. Hois is our new assistant pastor who has been here for three weeks. He holds a high church view and observes a fast every Friday. Meanwhile, Colonel Prospero is strongly opposed to any form of religious ceremony.
[22] "No, although he did mention it casually. However, this trouble was caused by Mrs. Price-Reed's one-pound note."
[23] Mrs. Price-Reed is a devout believer. During her son's memorial early morning prayer, she put a one-pound note into the donation bag. Later, when she saw the posted donation amount, she was distressed to find that the largest denomination mentioned was ten shillings.
[24] She complained to me about this, and I logically pointed out that she must have made a mistake.
[25] "We are no longer young," I tried to subtly change the subject of the conversation, "we must accept the punishment of aging."
[26] I didn't expect this to anger her. She said it was strange and she was surprised that I didn't see it that way. After that, she stormed off. I guess she went to complain to Colonel Prothero, who is always quick to make a fuss over things. He did make a fuss, unfortunately, it was Tuesday. I had a class at the mission school that morning, and this incident left me on edge and restless all day.
[27] "I think he must be happy," my wife tried to summarize her point fairly, "no one fusses over him, calls him dear pastor, sews him ugly slippers, or gives him Christmas socks. Even his wife and daughter are fed up with him. I think he's glad to find a place where he feels important."
[28] "He needn't be so rude," I was a bit agitated, "I think he may not realize what his words mean. He wants to review all the church accounts, he says, in case there are any signs of embezzlement – embezzlement, he actually used that word. Does he suspect me of embezzling church funds?"
[29] "No one will suspect you of doing anything, dear," said Gisella. "You are upright and blameless, and you can use this opportunity to prove it. I wish you would embezzle funds from the Gospel Propagation Society. I've always hated missionaries, and I still do."
[30] I wanted to reprimand her for this thought, but just then, Mary came in with a half-cooked rice pudding. I weakly protested, but Gisella said that the Japanese like undercooked rice, and that's why they are so smart.
[31] "I dare say," she said, "if you ate a rice pudding like this every day, all the way up to Sunday, your sermon would be very impressive."
[32] "I hope not," I shuddered.
[33] "Colonel Prothero will come over tomorrow night to check the accounts with me," I continued, "I have to prepare a speech for the British Church Men's Society, and I must have it ready today. I got distracted by Canon Shirley's book 'Reality' while researching, and I didn't get around to preparing the speech. What are you planning to do this afternoon, Giselda?"
[34] "Do my duty," Giselda said, "the duty of a pastor's wife. Four o'clock tea, listen to gossip."
[35] "Who will come?"
[36] Giselda rattled off a list of names, her face glowing with virtue.
[37] "Mrs. Price Reedley, Miss Weatherby, Miss Hartnell, and that dreadful Miss Maple."
[38] "I quite like Miss Maple," I said, "at least she has a sense of humor."
[39] "She's the most terrible old woman in the village," Giselda said. "She knows every detail of everything and makes the worst assumptions from it."
[40] As I've said, Giselda is much younger than me. People my age know that the worst is often the truth.
[41] "Oh, don't wait for me to have tea, Giselda," Dennis said.
[42] "Ugh!" Giselda exclaimed.
[43] "Well, you see, the Prothero's really did invite me to play tennis today."
[44] "Ugh!" Giselda exclaimed again.
[45] Dennis wisely left, and Giselda and I went into the study together.
[46] "What shall we have for tea?" Giselle asked, sitting on my desk, "I think Mr. Stone and Miss Crum will come, and perhaps Mrs. Lestrange. I went to see her yesterday, but she was out. Yes, we should invite Mrs. Lestrange to tea. I mean, she just came here so mysteriously, rented a house, and hardly ever goes out. It's all too mysterious, isn't it? It makes me think of detective stories. You know, the kind where 'the pale, beautiful woman is who is she? What is her past? No one knows. She has a faint aura of doom.' Dr. Hedock might know something about her."
[47] "I think you've read too many detective novels, Giselle," I said mildly.
[48] "And you?" she retorted, "One day, you were here writing a sermon, and I was looking for 'The Staircase Bloodstain.' I finally came in to ask you if you had seen it, and what happened?"
[49] My face reddened.
[50] "I just picked it up to flip through it, and something caught my attention, so…"
[51] "I know what those sentences are," Greta said, "Next, an unusual thing happened — Greta stood up, walked across the room, and kissed her elderly husband affectionately." She acted out the scene.
[52] "Is that unusual?" I asked.
[53] "Of course," Greta said, "Len, don't you understand? I could have married a cabinet minister, a baron, a wealthy company founder, or one of the three lieutenants and the charming waste. Instead, I chose you. Don't you find that surprising?"
[54] "Indeed, I am surprised," I replied, "I've never understood why you married me!"
[55] Greta burst out laughing.
[56] "Marriage to you makes me feel powerful," she murmured, "Others only see me as beautiful, of course, and it's a pleasure to have me. But you're different. You're the kind of person I despise and disagree with the most, yet you couldn't resist my charms. My vanity makes me irresistible to such things. Being a secret, happy sin is much better than being a feather on a hat. I've always been so terrifying, always making you angry and upset, but you still love me madly. Don't you, Len?"
[57] "Of course, I do love you, dear."
[58] "Oh! Len, you adore me. Remember that day when I stayed in town and sent you a telegram, but you didn't receive it because the postmistress's sister was giving birth to twins, and she forgot to send it. Oh, you were so scared that day, you even reported it to Scotland Yard, causing quite a commotion."
[59] That was something I didn't want to remember. I was so stupid that day. I said:
[60] "If you don't mind, dear, I'd like to finish writing my speech."
[61] Giselda sighed discontentedly, messed up my hair and smoothed it out, saying:
[62] "You don't deserve me. You really don't deserve me. I'm going to have an affair with an artist. I will do it, for sure. Think about the rumors that will spread through the parish then."
[63] "That's enough," I said mildly.
[64] Greta burst into laughter, gave me a flying kiss, and then walked through the French windows and left the study.

He had had a few arguments with the Colonel, but it was interesting that he had invited Lethis to witness the excavation.
[106] Suddenly, I remembered that Letitia Prothero was a bit eccentric. I wondered how she could get along with an archaeologist and his secretary, Miss Crum. Miss Crum was twenty-five years old, healthy, energetic, and rosy-cheeked, with a loud and noisy mouth that seemed to contain more teeth than it could hold.

[190][1] Parthian shot (from the ancient Parthian cavalry's use of a feigned retreat to launch a backstabbing attack), a parting shot fired when retreating. Now commonly used to refer to a final insult or criticism made before leaving a situation.

[213] As usual, there were few people at the Wednesday evening prayer. However, when I emerged from the vestry, dressed in my robes, I saw a woman standing in the empty church, gazing at a window. The church had ancient, exquisite stained glass windows, and the building itself was worth admiring. As I approached, she turned to face me, and I realized it was Mrs. Lestrange.
[214] We both hesitated for a moment, and I spoke first:
[215] "I hope you enjoyed our little church."
[216] "I was admiring the altar screen," she said.
[217] Her voice was deep and pleasant, with a unique timbre and clear enunciation. She continued:
[218] "I'm sorry I didn't see your wife yesterday."
[219] We talked about the church for a while longer. It was clear that she was a well-educated woman with knowledge of the church's history and architecture. We left the church together and took a stroll along a path that led past her house. When we reached her doorstep, she invited us in with a friendly gesture:
[220] "Come in and see how I've decorated my home."
[221] I accepted her invitation. The house had once belonged to a British colonel who had lived in India, and I breathed a sigh of relief when I saw that the brass dining table and Burmese statue were gone. The rooms were simply furnished but displayed a refined taste, giving a sense of harmony and tranquility.
[222] However, I grew increasingly perplexed as to why a woman like Mrs. Lestrange would come to St. Mary Mead. Clearly, she was a well-connected and sophisticated woman, yet it seemed strange that she would choose to live in the countryside. Her preferences were too peculiar.
[223] The living room was well-lit, and for the first time, I had the opportunity to observe her closely.
[224] This woman was tall, with hair that was a shade of gold with a hint of red. Her eyebrows and lashes were black, but I couldn't tell if it was natural or artificial. If it was the latter, her makeup skills were impressive. At rest, her face was a mystery. She had the most extraordinary eyes – when they were hidden in shadow, her irises appeared almost golden.
[225] She was elegantly dressed and composed, without any pretense. This woman was clearly well-educated, yet there was something elusive and confusing about her. She was a mystery, and I suddenly recalled the word Greta had used – ominous. Of course, it sounded absurd – but was it really that absurd? An unwelcome thought occurred to me: "This woman could do anything."

[2810] That night, as I climbed onto the podium, my mood was strange.
[2811] The church was unusually crowded. I couldn't believe the news of Hoise's sermon attracted so many people. Hoise's sermon was dull and doctrinaire. If the news had spread that I would take his place, it wouldn't have attracted these people. Because my sermon is not only dull but also pedantic. I can't attribute it to religious faith.
[2812] According to my inference, everyone who came here wanted to see who was here, and if possible, exchange some gossip after the sermon in the church porch.
[2813] Hedlock was also in the church, a rare visitor, and Lawrence Redding. I was surprised to see Hoise's pale and tense face beside Lawrence. Anne Prothero also came, who usually attends the evening prayer on Sundays, but I didn't expect her to come today. I was amazed to see Littice. Sunday morning is mandatory for church services - in this respect, Colonel Prothero is very stubborn, but I have never seen Littice attend the evening service.
[2814] Gladys Crum was also there, her youth and health contrasting sharply with the shriveled old maids around her. I think the blurred figure that entered the church late and sat at the back was probably Mrs. Lestrange.
[2815] As I said, Mrs. Price Reedley, Miss Harnell, Miss Weatherby, and Miss Maple were all present. All the villagers were there, almost no one absent. I can't remember when the church was so crowded.
[2816] The crowd was a strange thing. That night, the church was like a magnet, and I was the first to feel its influence.
[2817] Generally speaking, I would prepare my sermon in advance. My preparation was very thorough, but no one knew better than I did about its shortcomings.
[2818] Tonight, I had to give an extemporaneous sermon. I looked down at a face full of excitement and suddenly had a crazy idea. I was no longer God's shepherd but an actor. I was facing a crowd of spectators, and I wanted to move them - even, I felt I had the power to move them.
[2819] What I did that night didn't make me proud. I didn't believe in emotional religious revivalism. However, that night, I played the role of a rambling, roaring evangelist.
[2820] I spoke slowly.
[2821] I came not to call the righteous, but sinners to repentance.
[2822] I repeated this sentence twice. I heard my own voice, loud and clear, unlike my usual Leonard Clement voice.
[2823] I saw Greta, sitting in the front row, lift her head in surprise, and Dennis mirrored her action.
[2824] I held my breath for a moment, then let myself get carried away.
[2825] The congregation's emotions were at an extreme low, which meant the opportunity was ripe. So I did it. I exhorted the sinner to repentance. I stirred up my emotions, letting myself enter a state of ecstasy, extending my hand of condemnation again and again, reiterating this sentence:
[2826] "I say to you…"
[2827] Each time, sighs and gasps could be heard from different corners of the church.
[2828] The crowd's emotions were such a strange and terrible thing.
[2829] I concluded my sermon with this beautiful yet stern phrase — perhaps the sternest words in the entire Bible:
[2830] Tonight, your soul must be given…
[2831] This is a strange, fleeting state of possession. Back at the parsonage, I returned to my dim, vague self. I found Giselda pale. She put her hand in my arm.
[2832] "Lorenzo," she said, "you were terrible tonight. I – I don't like it. I've never heard you preach like that."

[2855] I was curious to know what Miss Marple was going to talk about. Among all the parishioners, I considered her the most intelligent. [2856] If I ever decided to start a career in deception, I would be afraid of Miss Maple.

I am not a punctual person, but I am an organized person, and I like to keep things in order.
[2858] Exactly at nine thirty, a soft knock came at the window. I got up to let Miss Maple in.
[2859] She was wrapped in a beautiful Shetland shawl, looking so old and frail. She came in, flustered and almost incoherent:
[2860] "Let me, you're so kind... dear Gileselda is so kind... Raymond appreciates her - he always calls her the perfect Greuze... No, I don't want a footstool."

[2862] "You must be wondering why - why I should be so interested in all this. You may think it's not a woman's job. No, I want to explain if I can."
[2863] She paused for a moment, her cheeks turning pink.
[2864] "You see," she began, "a person like me, living all alone in a remote corner, has to have some hobby. Of course, I can do tapestry, read The Guide and The Illustrated London News, and do a little sketching, but my hobby - my long-standing hobby - is studying human nature. Human nature is so varied, so fascinating. Of course, in a small village, there's not much to distract one, and we have plenty of opportunity to master one particular skill. So, I began to classify people, very clearly, treating them like flowers, grouping them by species, variety, and genus. Of course, there are occasional mistakes, but they become fewer as time goes on. Then I do experiments on myself, finding a little problem - say, the exquisite shrimp gills that pleased Giles so much - a trivial puzzle that's hard to understand before it's solved. Or, for example, changing cough lozenges, the butcher's wife's umbrella. The last thing is meaningless, unless we assume that the butcher's wife and the grocer's wife were behaving improperly, and, as it turned out, they were. You see, applying one's own judgment and finding oneself right is such a fascinating thing."
[2865] "I believe you're usually right," I said with a smile.

[2867] "You mean, it's a matter of relativity," I said slowly. "I admit it's—logically the same, but I don't know if it truly is."

In fact, the only way to compare this person with others you know or meet is to realize that there are few drastically different types. "
[2869] "You scare me," I said. "I feel like I'm under a microscope."

[3240] Needless to say, Miss Marple's plan succeeded. Lawrence Redding was not innocent, and hinting that someone had seen him switch the capsules did indeed make him "do a foolish thing." That is the mark of guilt.
[3241] Of course, he would be treated specially. I suppose his first instinct would be to flee. But he had to consider his accomplice. He couldn't just disappear without a trace, and he dared not wait until morning. So that night, he went to the old wing of the church, followed by two of Major Melchett's trusted men. He threw stones at Anne Prothero's window, waking her up, and after a few hushed words, she came down to talk to him. It was undoubtedly safer outside than inside – because Leithe might be awake. But that way, the two policemen overheard their entire conversation. The truth came out. Miss Marple had anticipated everything.
[3242] Everyone knew that Lawrence Redding and Anne Prothero would be interrogated. I'll just mention that the credit goes to Inspector Slack, whose enthusiasm and cunning ultimately led to the capture of the culprit. Of course, they didn't mention Miss Marple's contribution. She must have been horrified at such a thing.
[3243] Before the interrogation, Leithe came to see me. She still drifted in like a ghostly apparition through the French windows. She told me that she had always believed her stepmother was an accomplice. Searching for the missing yellow beret was nothing more than an excuse to search the study. She held onto a glimmer of hope that she might find something the police had overlooked.
[3244] "You see," she said in a dreamy voice, "they don't hate her as much as I do. Hatred makes things easier."

[3250] I look up in surprise.

[3252] "You didn't guess, did you? Leitersenz is my mother. She - she's dying, you know. She wanted to see me, so she came here under an assumed name. Dr. Haddock helped her. He's been her old friend - he used to be very infatuated with her - you can tell! In a way, he still is. I believe that men always go crazy for my mother. Even now, she's still so alluring. Anyway, Dr. Haddock did everything he could to help her. She used a false name so that people here wouldn't talk about her in a disgusting way. She went to see her father that night, telling him that she was dying and that she had always wanted to see me once. My father is a beast! He said she had lost all rights and that I thought she was dead - as if I ever believed that lie! Men like my father can only see what's in front of their noses!
[3253] "But my mother is not the kind of person to easily give up. She just thought it was proper to see my father first, but when her request was rudely refused, she sent me a note. So I left the tennis party early and met her at the end of the path at 6:15. We only met briefly and made arrangements for the next meeting. We parted ways before 6:30. I was afraid the police would suspect her of being the killer. After all, she had a grudge against him. That's why I found her portrait in the attic and destroyed it. I was afraid the police would investigate and find the portrait and recognize her. Dr. Haddock was also terrified. I think he really thought it was her! My mother is a - a person who doesn't care about the consequences. She does whatever she wants."
[3254] She stopped.
